<p><b>Abraham Lincoln</b> (February 12, 1809&#160;– April 15, 1865) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">16th President of the United States</a>, serving from March 1861 until <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Assassination of Abraham Lincoln">his assassination</a> in April 1865. He successfully led the country through its greatest constitutional, military and moral crisis—the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War">American Civil War</a>—preserving the Union while ending slavery and promoting economic modernization. Reared in a poor family on the western frontier, he was mostly self-educated. He became a country lawyer, an <a href="/wiki/Illinois">Illinois</a> <a href="/wiki/Illinois_House_of_Representatives" title="Illinois House of Representatives">state legislator</a>, and a one-term member of the United States <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> but failed in two attempts at a seat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate">United States Senate</a>. He was an affectionate, though often absent, husband and father of four children.</p>

<p>Lincoln was an outspoken opponent of the expansion of <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States">slavery in the United States</a>, which he deftly articulated in his campaign debates and speeches.<sup id="cite_ref-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-0"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> As a result, he secured the Republican nomination and was <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1860" title="United States presidential election, 1860">elected president in 1860</a>. After war began, following declarations of <a href="/wiki/Secession_in_the_United_States" title="Secession in the United States">secession</a> by southern slave states, he concentrated on both the military and political dimensions of the war effort, seeking to reunify the nation. He vigorously exercised unprecedented war powers, including the arrest and detention without trial of thousands of suspected secessionists. He issued his <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a> in 1863 and promoted the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Thirteenth Amendment</a> to the United States Constitution, abolishing slavery.</p>

<p>Lincoln closely supervised the war effort, especially the selection of top generals, including <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>. He brought leaders of various factions of his party into his cabinet and pressured them to cooperate. He defused a confrontation with Britain in the <a href="/wiki/Trent_Affair" title="Trent Affair"><i>Trent</i> affair</a> late in 1861. Under his leadership, the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> took control of the <a href="/wiki/Border_states_(American_Civil_War)" title="Border states (American Civil War)">border slave states</a> at the start of the war and tried repeatedly to capture the Confederate capital at Richmond. Each time a general failed, Lincoln substituted another until finally Grant succeeded in 1865. A shrewd politician deeply involved with patronage and power issues in each state, he reached out to <a href="/wiki/War_Democrats">War Democrats</a> and managed his own reelection in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1864" title="United States presidential election, 1864">1864 presidential election</a>.</p>

<p>As the leader of the moderate faction of the Republican party, Lincoln came under attack from all sides. <a href="/wiki/Radical_Republicans">Radical Republicans</a> wanted harsher treatment of the South, Democrats desired more compromise, and secessionists saw him as their enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln fought back with patronage, by pitting his opponents against each other, and by appealing to the American people with his powers of oratory;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> for example, his <a href="/wiki/Gettysburg_Address">Gettysburg Address</a> of 1863 became one of the most quoted speeches in American history. It was an iconic statement of America's dedication to the principles of nationalism, equal rights, liberty, and democracy. At the close of the war, Lincoln held a moderate view of <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction era of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Reconstruction</a>, seeking to speedily reunite the nation through a policy of generous reconciliation in the face of lingering and bitter divisiveness. Just six days after the decisive surrender of the commanding general of the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_army" class="mw-redirect" title="Confederate army">Confederate army</a>, Lincoln was shot and killed, the first U.S. president to be assassinated. Lincoln has consistently been <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_United_States_Presidents#Scholar_survey_results" title="Historical rankings of United States Presidents" class="mw-redirect">ranked by scholars</a> as one of the greatest U.S. presidents.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>

<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_and_career_of_Abraham_Lincoln">Early life and career of Abraham Lincoln</a></div>
<p>Abraham Lincoln was born February 12, 1809, the second child of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Lincoln">Thomas Lincoln</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Lincoln">Nancy Lincoln</a> (née Hanks), in a one-room <a href="/wiki/Log_cabin">log cabin</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Sinking_Spring_Farm" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinking Spring Farm">Sinking Spring Farm</a> in southeast <a href="/wiki/Hardin_County,_Kentucky" title="Hardin County, Kentucky">Hardin County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kentucky">Kentucky</a><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> (now <a href="/wiki/LaRue_County,_Kentucky" title="LaRue County, Kentucky">LaRue County</a>).</p>

<p>Little is known about Lincoln's ancestors. Historical investigations have traced his family back to <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Lincoln">Samuel Lincoln</a>, an apprentice weaver who arrived in <a href="/wiki/Hingham,_Massachusetts" title="Hingham, Massachusetts">Hingham</a>, Massachusetts, from <a href="/wiki/Norfolk">Norfolk</a>, England, in 1637.<sup id="cite_ref-pessen-1984-p24-25_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pessen-1984-p24-25-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-donald-1996-p20_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-donald-1996-p20-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Lincoln himself was only able to trace his heritage back as far as his paternal grandfather and namesake, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln_(captain)" title="Abraham Lincoln (captain)">Abraham Lincoln</a>, a local militia captain and substantial landholder with an inherited 200-acre (81&#160;ha) estate in <a href="/wiki/Rockingham_County,_Virginia">Rockingham County, Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pessen-1984-p24-25_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pessen-1984-p24-25-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> The elder Abraham later moved his family from Rockingham County, Virginia, to <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_County,_Kentucky">Jefferson County, Kentucky</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-pessen-1984-p24-25_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pessen-1984-p24-25-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-white-12_13_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white-12_13-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> where he was ambushed and killed in an Indian raid in 1786 with his children <a href="/wiki/Mordecai_Lincoln" title="Mordecai Lincoln">Mordecai</a>, Josiah, and Thomas looking on.<sup id="cite_ref-white-12_13_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white-12_13-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Mordecai's marksmanship with a rifle saved Thomas from the same fate. As the eldest son, by law Mordecai inherited his father's entire estate.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>Thomas became a respected citizen of rural Kentucky. He bought and sold several farms, including the Sinking Spring Farm. The family belonged to a <a href="/wiki/Separate_Baptists">Separate Baptists</a> church, which had high moral standards and opposed alcohol, dancing, and slavery,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> though Thomas, as an adult, never joined a church. Thomas "enjoyed considerable status" in Kentucky, where he sat on juries, appraised estates, served on country patrols, and guarded prisoners. By the time his son Abraham was born, Thomas owned two 600-acre (240&#160;ha) farms, several town lots, livestock, and horses. He was among the richest men in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-pessen-1984-p24-25_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pessen-1984-p24-25-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1816, the Lincoln family lost their lands because of a faulty title and made a new start in <a href="/wiki/Perry_County,_Indiana" title="Perry County, Indiana">Perry County</a>, Indiana (now <a href="/wiki/Spencer_County,_Indiana" title="Spencer County, Indiana">Spencer County</a>). Lincoln later noted that this move was "partly on account of slavery" but mainly due to <a href="/wiki/Land_title" class="mw-redirect" title="Land title">land title</a> difficulties.<sup id="cite_ref-sandberg-20_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sandberg-20-12"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abe-Lincoln-Birthplace-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A log house on display inside a larger building." src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Abe-Lincoln-Birthplace-2.jpg/220px-Abe-Lincoln-Birthplace-2.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abe-Lincoln-Birthplace-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A log cabin replica at the <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln_Birthplace_National_Historical_Park">Abraham Lincoln Birthplace National Historical Park</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Lincoln was nine, his 34-year-old mother died of <a href="/wiki/Milk_sickness">milk sickness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-donald-1996-p30-33_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-donald-1996-p30-33-13"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> His older sister, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Lincoln_Grigsby" title="Sarah Lincoln Grigsby">Sarah (Grigsby)</a>, died while giving birth at a young age.<sup id="cite_ref-donald-1996-p20_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-donald-1996-p20-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Soon after, his father married <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Bush_Lincoln" title="Sarah Bush Lincoln">Sarah Bush Johnston</a>, with whom Lincoln became very close and whom he called "Mother."<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> However, he became increasingly distant from his father. Lincoln regretted his father's lack of education and did not like the hard labor associated with frontier life. Still, he willingly took responsibility for all chores expected of him as a male in the household and became an adept axeman in his work building rail fences. Lincoln also agreed with the customary obligation of a son to give his father all earnings from work done outside the home until age 21.<sup id="cite_ref-donald-1996-p30-33_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-donald-1996-p30-33-13"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> In later years, he occasionally loaned his father money.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>In 1830, fearing a milk sickness outbreak, the family settled on public land in <a href="/wiki/Macon_County,_Illinois" title="Macon County, Illinois">Macon County</a>, Illinois.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1831, when his father relocated the family to a <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Log_Cabin_State_Historic_Site" title="Lincoln Log Cabin State Historic Site">new homestead</a> in <a href="/wiki/Coles_County,_Illinois" title="Coles County, Illinois">Coles County</a>, Illinois, 22-year-old Lincoln struck out on his own, canoeing down the <a href="/wiki/Sangamon_River">Sangamon River</a> to the village of <a href="/wiki/New_Salem,_Menard_County,_Illinois" title="New Salem, Menard County, Illinois">New Salem</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sangamon_County,_Illinois" title="Sangamon County, Illinois">Sangamon County</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> In the spring of 1831, hired by New Salem businessman <a href="/wiki/Denton_Offutt">Denton Offutt</a> and accompanied by friends, he took goods by flatboat from New Salem to <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans">New Orleans</a> via the Sangamon, <a href="/wiki/Illinois_River" title="Illinois River">Illinois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi</a> rivers. After arriving in New Orleans—and witnessing slavery firsthand—he walked back home.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>Lincoln's formal education consisted of approximately 18 months of classes from several itinerant teachers; he was mostly self-educated and was an avid reader.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> He attained a reputation for brawn and audacity after a very competitive wrestling match to which he was challenged by the renowned leader of a group of ruffians, "the Clary's Grove boys."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> His family and neighbors considered him to be lazy.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln avoided hunting and fishing out of an aversion to killing animals.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_family">Marriage and family</span></h2>
<div class="rellink boilerplate further">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sexuality_of_Abraham_Lincoln">Sexuality of Abraham Lincoln</a>; <a href="/wiki/Medical_and_mental_health_of_Abraham_Lincoln">Medical and mental health of Abraham Lincoln</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mary_Todd_Lincoln_1846-1847_restored_cropped.png" class="image"><img alt="Black and white photo of Mary Todd Lincoln's shoulders and head" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Mary_Todd_Lincoln_1846-1847_restored_cropped.png/170px-Mary_Todd_Lincoln_1846-1847_restored_cropped.png" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mary_Todd_Lincoln_1846-1847_restored_cropped.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Mary Todd Lincoln, wife of Abraham Lincoln, at age 28</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lincoln's first romantic interest was <a href="/wiki/Ann_Rutledge">Ann Rutledge</a>, whom he met when he first moved to New Salem; by 1835, they were in a relationship but not formally engaged. Rutledge wanted to notify a former love before "consummating the engagement to Mr. L. with marriage." She died, however, on August 25, most likely of <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever">typhoid fever</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>In the early 1830s, he met Mary Owens from Kentucky when she was visiting her sister. Late in 1836, Lincoln agreed to a match with Mary if she returned to New Salem. Mary did return in November 1836, and Lincoln courted her for a time; however, they both had second thoughts about their relationship. On August 16, 1837, Lincoln wrote Mary a letter from his law practice in Springfield, suggesting he would not blame her if she ended the relationship. She never replied and the courtship was over.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> For the next four years Lincoln boarded with his Kentucky gentry friend <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Speed" class="mw-redirect" title="Joshua Speed">Joshua Speed</a> in Springfield.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1840, Lincoln became engaged to <a href="/wiki/Mary_Todd_Lincoln" title="Mary Todd Lincoln">Mary Todd</a>, who was from a wealthy slave-holding family in <a href="/wiki/Lexington,_Kentucky" title="Lexington, Kentucky">Lexington</a>, Kentucky.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> They met in Springfield in December 1839<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-28"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> and were engaged the following December.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> A wedding was set for January 1, 1841, but the couple split as the wedding approached.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-28"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> They later met at a party and then married on November 4, 1842, in the Springfield mansion of Mary's married sister.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> While preparing for the nuptials and feeling reluctance again, Lincoln, when asked where he was going, replied, "To hell, I suppose."<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A%26TLincoln.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A seated Lincoln holding a book as his young son looks at it" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/A%26TLincoln.jpg/170px-A%26TLincoln.jpg" width="170" height="188" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A%26TLincoln.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mathew_Brady">Mathew Brady</a> photo (1864) depicts President Lincoln reading a book with his youngest son, Tad.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1844, the couple bought a house in Springfield near Lincoln's law office.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Mary Todd Lincoln worked diligently in their home, assuming household duties which had been performed for her in her own family. She also made efficient use of the limited funds available from her husband's law practice.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> One evening, Mary asked Lincoln four times to restart the fire and, getting no reaction as he was absorbed in his reading, she grabbed a piece of firewood and rapped him on the head.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> The Lincolns had a budding family with the birth of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Todd_Lincoln">Robert Todd Lincoln</a> in 1843 and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Baker_Lincoln">Edward Baker Lincoln</a> in 1846. According to a house girl, Abraham "was remarkably fond of children,"<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Lincolns were not thought to be strict with their children.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>Robert was the only child of the Lincolns to live past the age of 18. Edward Lincoln died on February 1, 1850, in Springfield, likely of <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> The Lincolns' grief over this loss was somewhat assuaged by the birth of <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace_Lincoln" title="William Wallace Lincoln">William "Willie" Wallace Lincoln</a> nearly 11 months later, on December 21. However, Willie died of a fever at the age of 11 on February 20, 1862, in Washington, D.C., during President Lincoln's first term.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> The Lincolns' fourth son, <a href="/wiki/Tad_Lincoln" title="Tad Lincoln">Thomas "Tad" Lincoln</a>, was born on April 4, 1853, and outlived his father but died at the age of 18 on July 16, 1871, in Chicago.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>The death of their sons had profound effects on both parents. Later in life, Mary struggled with the stresses of losing her husband and sons, and Robert Lincoln committed her to a mental health asylum in 1875.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Abraham Lincoln suffered from "<a href="/wiki/Melancholy" class="mw-redirect" title="Melancholy">melancholy</a>", a condition which now may be referred to as <a href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" class="mw-redirect" title="Clinical depression">clinical depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Lincoln's father-in-law was based in Lexington, Kentucky; he and others of the Todd family were either slave owners or slave traders. Lincoln was close to the Todds, and he and his family occasionally visited the Todd estate in Lexington; Lincoln's connections in Lexington could have accelerated his ambitions, but he remained in Illinois, where, to his liking, slavery was almost nonexistent.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career_and_military_service">Early career and military service</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_and_career_of_Abraham_Lincoln">Early life and career of Abraham Lincoln</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln_in_the_Black_Hawk_War">Abraham Lincoln in the Black Hawk War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abe_Lincoln_young.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Thin man looking to the right wearing a bow tie." src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Abe_Lincoln_young.jpg/170px-Abe_Lincoln_young.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abe_Lincoln_young.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A sketch of young Abraham Lincoln</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1832, at age 23, Lincoln and a partner bought a small general store on credit in New Salem, Illinois. Although the economy was booming in the region, the business struggled and Lincoln eventually sold his share. When his partner later died, Lincoln became liable for a $1,000 debt. Unable to pay, he was forced to declare bankruptcy and did not finish repaying his creditors for another 17 years.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> That same year he began his political career with a campaign for the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_General_Assembly">Illinois General Assembly</a>. He had attained local popularity and could draw crowds as a natural raconteur in New Salem, though he lacked an education, powerful friends, and money. He advocated navigational improvements on the Sangamon River.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Before the election, he served briefly as a captain in the Illinois militia during the <a href="/wiki/Black_Hawk_War">Black Hawk War</a>, although he never saw combat. Lincoln returned from the militia and was able to campaign for the August 6 election. At 6&#160;feet 4&#160;inches (1.93&#160;m), he was tall and "strong enough to intimidate any rival." At his first speech, he grabbed an antagonist by his "neck and the seat of his trousers" and threw him. Lincoln finished eighth out of thirteen candidates (the top four were elected), though he got 277 of the 300 votes cast in the New Salem precinct.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>Lincoln served as New Salem's postmaster and later as county surveyor, after more dedicated self education.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1834, he won election to the state legislature after a bipartisan campaign, though he ran as a <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> He then decided to become a lawyer and began teaching himself law by reading Blackstone's <i><a href="/wiki/Commentaries_on_the_Laws_of_England">Commentaries on the Laws of England</a></i> and other law books. Of his learning method, Lincoln stated: "I studied with nobody."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_the_bar_in_the_United_States" title="Admission to the bar in the United States">Admitted to the bar</a> in 1836,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> he moved to Springfield, Illinois and began to practice law under <a href="/wiki/John_T._Stuart">John T. Stuart</a>, Mary Todd's cousin.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln became an able and successful lawyer with a reputation as a formidable adversary during cross-examinations and closing arguments. In 1841, he partnered with Stephen Logan until 1844, when he began his practice with <a href="/wiki/William_Herndon_(lawyer)" title="William Herndon (lawyer)">William Herndon</a>, whom Lincoln thought "a studious young man."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> He served four successive terms in the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_House_of_Representatives">Illinois House of Representatives</a> as a Whig representative from Sangamon County.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>In the 1835–1836 legislative session, he voted to expand <a href="/wiki/Suffrage">suffrage</a> to white males, whether landowners or not.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> He was known for his "free soil" stance of opposing both slavery and <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism">abolitionism</a>. He first articulated this in 1837, saying, "Institution of slavery is founded on both injustice and bad policy, but the promulgation of abolition doctrines tends rather to increase than abate its evils."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> He closely followed <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay">Henry Clay</a> in supporting the <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society">American Colonization Society</a> program of making the abolition of slavery practical by helping the freed slaves return to <a href="/wiki/Liberia">Liberia</a> in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_national_politics">Early national politics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abelincoln1846.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="Middle aged clean shaven Lincoln from the hips up." src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Abelincoln1846.jpeg/170px-Abelincoln1846.jpeg" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abelincoln1846.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A shaven Lincoln is revealed in 1846 or 1847.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From the early 1830s, Lincoln was a steadfast Whig and professed to friends in 1861, "I have always been an old-line Henry Clay Whig."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> The party favored economic modernization in banking, railroads, and internal improvements and espoused urbanization as well as protective tariffs, and Lincoln supported these positions.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>In 1846, Lincoln was elected to the U.S. House of Representatives, where he served one two-year term.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> He was the only Whig in the Illinois delegation, but he showed his party loyalty by participating in almost all votes and making speeches that echoed the party line.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln developed a plan to abolish slavery in the District of Columbia with compensation for the owners and a popular vote on the matter, but he dropped it when he failed to gather sufficient Whig supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln also spoke against the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican–American War</a>, which he attributed to <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">President Polk</a>'s desire for "military glory—that attractive rainbow, that rises in showers of blood."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>Lincoln articulated his opposition to Polk by drafting and introducing his <a href="/wiki/Spot_Resolutions">Spot Resolutions</a>. The war had begun with a violent confrontation on territory disputed by Mexico and the U.S., but Polk insisted that Mexican soldiers had "invaded <i>our territory</i> and shed the blood of our fellow-citizens on our <i>own soil</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Basler1pp199.E2.80.94202_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Basler1pp199.E2.80.94202-66"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln demanded that Polk show Congress the exact spot on which blood had been shed and prove that the spot was on American soil.<sup id="cite_ref-Basler1pp199.E2.80.94202_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Basler1pp199.E2.80.94202-66"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress never enacted the resolution or even debated it, the national papers ignored it, and it resulted in a loss of political support for Lincoln in his district. One Illinois newspaper derisively nicknamed him "spotty Lincoln."<sup id="cite_ref-McGovern.2C_p._33_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGovern.2C_p._33-67"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln later came to regret some of his statements, especially his attack on the presidential war-making powers.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>Realizing Clay was unlikely to win the presidency, Lincoln, who had pledged in 1846 to serve only one term in the House,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> devoted his efforts in support of General <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a> for the Whig nomination in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1848" title="United States presidential election, 1848">1848 presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Taylor won and Lincoln hoped to be appointed Commissioner of the General Land Office, but that lucrative patronage job went to an Illinois rival.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> The administration offered him the consolation prize of secretary or governor of the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Territory">Oregon Territory</a>. Since the territory was a Democratic stronghold, acceptance would have ended his legal and political career in Illinois, so he declined in favor of a reprisal of his law practice.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prairie_lawyer">Prairie lawyer</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_Lincoln_By_Charles_Keck.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Young_Lincoln_By_Charles_Keck.JPG/220px-Young_Lincoln_By_Charles_Keck.JPG" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Young_Lincoln_By_Charles_Keck.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The young Lincoln in sculpture at Senn Park Chicago</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lincoln returned to practicing law in Springfield, handling "every kind of business that could come before a prairie lawyer."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Twice a year for sixteen years, ten weeks at a time – appeared in county seats in the midstate region when the county courts were in session.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln handled many transportation cases in the midst of the nation's western expansion, particularly the conflicts arising from the operation of river barges under the many new railroad bridges. As a riverboat man, Lincoln initially favored those interests but ultimately represented whoever hired him.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> His reputation grew, and he appeared before the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>, arguing a case involving a canal boat that sank after hitting a bridge.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1849, he received a <a href="/wiki/Patent">patent</a> for a flotation device for the movement of boats in shallow water.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> The idea was never commercialized, but Lincoln is the only president to hold a patent.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>In 1851, he represented <a href="/wiki/Alton_Railroad" title="Alton Railroad">Alton &amp; Sangamon Railroad</a> in a dispute with one of its shareholders, James A. Barret, who had refused to pay the balance on his pledge to buy shares in the railroad on the grounds that the company had changed its original train route.<sup id="cite_ref-Donald_p._155_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donald_p._155-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln successfully argued that the railroad company was not bound by its original charter in existence at the time of Barret's pledge; the charter was amended in the public interest to provide a newer, superior, and less expensive route, and the corporation retained the right to demand Barret's payment. The decision by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Illinois" title="Supreme Court of Illinois">Illinois Supreme Court</a> has been cited by numerous other courts in the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-Donald_p._155_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donald_p._155-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln appeared before the Illinois Supreme Court in 175 cases, in 51 as sole counsel, of which 31 were decided in his favor.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> From 1853 to 1860, another of Lincoln's largest clients was the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Central_Railroad">Illinois Central Railroad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>Lincoln's most notable criminal trial occurred in 1858 when he defended <a href="/wiki/William_%22Duff%22_Armstrong">William "Duff" Armstrong</a>, who was on trial for the murder of James Preston Metzker.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> The case is famous for Lincoln's use of a fact established by <a href="/wiki/Judicial_notice">judicial notice</a> in order to challenge the credibility of an eyewitness. After an opposing witness testified seeing the crime in the moonlight, Lincoln produced a <a href="/wiki/Farmers%27_Almanac">Farmers' Almanac</a> showing the moon was at a low angle, drastically reducing visibility. Based on this evidence, Armstrong was acquitted.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln rarely raised objections in the courtroom. In 1859, however, in another celebrated case, in which he defended Peachy Harrison, who was accused of stabbing another to death, Lincoln angrily protested the judge's decision to exclude evidence favorable to his client. Instead of holding Lincoln in contempt of court as was expected, the judge, a Democrat, reversed his ruling, allowing the evidence and acquitting Harrison.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_politics_1854.E2.80.931860">Republican politics 1854–1860</span></h2>
<p>Lincoln returned to politics to oppose the pro-slavery <a href="/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska_Act">Kansas–Nebraska Act</a> (1854); this law repealed the slavery-restricting <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> (1820). Senior Senator <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> of Illinois had incorporated <a href="/wiki/Popular_sovereignty">popular sovereignty</a> into the Act. Douglas' provision, which Lincoln opposed, specified the people have the right to determine locally whether to allow slavery in their territory rather than have such a decision imposed on them by the national Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_by_Alexander_Helser,_1860-crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Head shot of older, clean shaven Lincoln" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Abraham_Lincoln_by_Alexander_Helser%2C_1860-crop.jpg/170px-Abraham_Lincoln_by_Alexander_Helser%2C_1860-crop.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_by_Alexander_Helser,_1860-crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A. Helser filmed Lincoln in 1860.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On October 16, 1854, in his "<a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln_Peoria_speech" title="Abraham Lincoln Peoria speech">Peoria Speech</a>," Lincoln declared his opposition to slavery, which he repeated en route to the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Speaking in his Kentucky accent, with a very powerful voice,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> he said the Kansas Act had a "'declared' indifference, but as I must think, a covert 'real' zeal for the spread of slavery. I cannot but hate it. I hate it because of the monstrous injustice of slavery itself. I hate it because it deprives our republican example of its just influence in the world..."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>In late 1854, Lincoln ran as a Whig for the U.S. Senate seat from Illinois. At that time, senators were elected by the state legislature.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> After leading in the first six rounds of voting in the Illinois assembly, his support began to dwindle, and Lincoln instructed his backers to vote for <a href="/wiki/Lyman_Trumbull">Lyman Trumbull</a>, who defeated opponent <a href="/wiki/Joel_Aldrich_Matteson">Joel Aldrich Matteson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> The Whigs had been irreparably split by the Kansas–Nebraska Act. Lincoln said, "I think I am a Whig, but others say there are no Whigs, and that I am an abolitionist, even though I do no more than oppose the <i>extension</i> of slavery." Drawing on remnants of the old Whig party, and on disenchanted Free Soil, Liberty, and Democratic party members, he was instrumental in forging the shape of the new Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> At the Republican convention in 1856, Lincoln placed second in the contest to become the party's candidate for vice president.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>In 1857–58, Douglas broke with President <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">Buchanan</a>, leading to a fight for control of the Democratic Party. Some eastern Republicans even favored the reelection of Douglas for the Senate in 1858, since he had led the opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Lecompton_Constitution">Lecompton Constitution</a>, which would have admitted Kansas as a <a href="/wiki/Slave_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave state">slave state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> In March 1857, the Supreme Court issued its controversial decision in <i><a href="/wiki/Dred_Scott_v._Sandford">Dred Scott v. Sandford</a></i>; Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Roger_B._Taney">Roger B. Taney</a> opined that blacks were not citizens, and derived no rights from the Declaration of Independence or Constitution. Lincoln, though strong in his disagreement with the Court's opinion, was as a lawyer unequivocal in his deference to the Court's authority. Lincoln historian <a href="/wiki/David_Herbert_Donald">David Herbert Donald</a> provides Lincoln's immediate reaction to the decision, showing his evolving position on slavery: "The authors of the Declaration of Independence never intended 'to say all were equal in color, size, intellect, moral developments, or social capacity', but they 'did consider all men created equal—equal in certain inalienable rights, among which are life, liberty, and the pursuit of happiness'."<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> After the state Republican party convention nominated him for the U.S. Senate in 1858, Lincoln then delivered <a href="/wiki/Lincoln%27s_House_Divided_Speech" title="Lincoln's House Divided Speech">his famous speech</a>: "&#160;'A house divided against itself cannot stand'.(<a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">Mark</a> 3:25) I believe this government cannot endure permanently half slave and half free. I do not expect the Union to be dissolved—I do not expect the house to fall—but I do expect it will cease to be divided. It will become all one thing, or all the other."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> The speech created an evocative image of the danger of disunion caused by the slavery debate, and rallied Republicans across the North.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> The stage was then set for the campaign for statewide election of the Illinois legislature which would, in turn, select Lincoln or Douglas as its U.S. Senator.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lincoln.E2.80.93Douglas_debates_and_Cooper_Union_speech">Lincoln–Douglas debates and Cooper Union speech</span></h3>
<p>The 1858 campaign featured the seven <a href="/wiki/Lincoln%E2%80%93Douglas_debates_of_1858">Lincoln–Douglas debates of 1858</a>, generally considered the most famous political debates in American history.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> The principals stood in stark contrast both physically and politically. Lincoln warned that "<a href="/wiki/The_Slave_Power">The Slave Power</a>" was threatening the values of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republicanism</a>, and accused Douglas of distorting the values of the Founding Fathers that <a href="/wiki/All_men_are_created_equal">all men are created equal</a>, while Douglas emphasized his <a href="/wiki/Freeport_Doctrine">Freeport Doctrine</a>, that local settlers were free to choose whether to allow slavery or not, and accused Lincoln of having joined the abolitionists.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> The debates had an atmosphere of a prize fight and drew huge crowds in the thousands. Lincoln stated Douglas's <a href="/wiki/Popular_sovereignty">popular sovereignty</a> theory was a threat to the nation's morality and that Douglas represented a conspiracy to extend slavery to free states. Douglas said that Lincoln was defying the authority of the U.S. Supreme Court and the Dred Scott decision.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lincoln_Douglas_Debates_1958_issue-4c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="4 cent stamp with a drawing of Lincoln giving a speech to a crowd." src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Lincoln_Douglas_Debates_1958_issue-4c.jpg/220px-Lincoln_Douglas_Debates_1958_issue-4c.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lincoln_Douglas_Debates_1958_issue-4c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
U.S. Postage, 1958 issue, commemorates the Lincoln-Douglas debates.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Though the Republican legislative candidates won more popular votes, the Democrats won more seats, and the legislature re-elected Douglas to the Senate. Despite the bitterness of the defeat for Lincoln, his articulation of the issues gave him a national political reputation.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 1859, Lincoln purchased the <i>Illinois Staats-Anzeiger,</i> a German-language newspaper which was consistently supportive; most of the state's 130,000 German Americans voted Democratic but there was Republican support that a German-language paper could mobilize.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>On February 27, 1860, New York party leaders invited Lincoln to give a <a href="/wiki/Cooper_Union_speech" title="Cooper Union speech">speech at Cooper Union</a> to a group of powerful Republicans. Lincoln argued that the Founding Fathers had little use for popular sovereignty and had repeatedly sought to restrict slavery. Lincoln insisted the moral foundation of the Republicans required opposition to slavery, and rejected any "groping for some middle ground between the right and the wrong."<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite his inelegant appearance—many in the audience thought him awkward and even ugly<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup>—Lincoln demonstrated an intellectual leadership that brought him into the front ranks of the party and into contention for the Republican presidential nomination. Journalist <a href="/wiki/Noah_Brooks">Noah Brooks</a> reported, "No man ever before made such an impression on his first appeal to a New York audience."<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> Donald described the speech as a "superb political move for an unannounced candidate, to appear in one rival's (<a href="/wiki/William_H._Seward">William H. Seward</a>) own state at an event sponsored by the second rival's (<a href="/wiki/Salmon_P._Chase">Salmon P. Chase</a>) loyalists, while not mentioning either by name during its delivery."<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> In response to an inquiry about his presidential intentions, Lincoln said, "The taste <i>is</i> in my mouth a little."<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1860_Presidential_nomination_and_election">1860 Presidential nomination and election</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1860">United States presidential election, 1860</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Rail_Candidate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Lincoln being carried by two men on a long board." src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/The_Rail_Candidate.jpg/220px-The_Rail_Candidate.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Rail_Candidate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
"The Rail Candidate"—Lincoln's 1860 candidacy is depicted as held up by the slavery issue—a slave on the left and party organization on the right.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On May 9–10, 1860, the Illinois Republican State Convention was held in <a href="/wiki/Decatur,_Illinois" title="Decatur, Illinois">Decatur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln's followers organized a campaign team led by David Davis, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Judd" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Judd">Norman Judd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Swett">Leonard Swett</a>, and Jesse DuBois, and Lincoln received his first endorsement to run for the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Exploiting the embellished legend of his frontier days with his father, Lincoln's supporters adopted the label of "The Rail Candidate."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> On May 18, at the <a href="/wiki/1860_Republican_National_Convention">1860 Republican National Convention</a> in Chicago, Lincoln became the Republican candidate on the third ballot, beating candidates such as William H. Seward and Salmon P. Chase.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> Former Democrat <a href="/wiki/Hannibal_Hamlin">Hannibal Hamlin</a> of Maine received the nomination for Vice President to balance the ticket. Lincoln's nomination has been attributed in part to his moderate views on slavery, as well as his support of internal improvements and the protective tariff. In terms of the actual balloting, Pennsylvania put him over the top. (Lincoln made known to Pennsylvania iron interests his support for protective tariffs.) Lincoln's managers had been adroitly focused on this delegation as well as the others, while following Lincoln's strong dictate to "Make no contracts that bind me."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>Most Republicans agreed with Lincoln that the North was the aggrieved party, as the Slave Power tightened its grasp on the national government with the Dred Scott decision and the presidency of James Buchanan.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Throughout the 1850s, Lincoln doubted the prospects of civil war, and his supporters rejected claims that his election would incite secession.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> Meanwhile, Douglas was selected as the candidate of the Northern Democrats, with <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Vespasian_Johnson">Herschel Vespasian Johnson</a> as the vice-presidential candidate. Delegates from 11 slave states walked out of the Democratic convention, disagreeing with Douglas's position on popular sovereignty, and ultimately selected <a href="/wiki/John_C._Breckinridge">John C. Breckinridge</a> as their candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>As Douglas and the other candidates went through with their campaigns, Lincoln was the only one of them who gave no speeches. Instead, he monitored the campaign closely and relied on the enthusiasm of the Republican Party. The party did the leg work that produced majorities across the North, and produced an abundance of campaign posters, leaflets, and newspaper editorials.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> There were thousands of Republican speakers who focused first on the party platform, and second on Lincoln's life story, emphasizing his childhood poverty. The goal was to demonstrate the superior power of "free labor," whereby a common farm boy could work his way to the top by his own efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> The Republican Party's production of campaign literature dwarfed the combined opposition; a <i>Chicago Tribune</i> writer produced a pamphlet that detailed Lincoln's life, and sold one million copies.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency">Presidency</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1860_election_and_secession">1860 election and secession</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Plot">Baltimore Plot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornerstone_Speech">Cornerstone Speech</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1860_Electoral_Map.png" class="image"><img alt="Map of the U.S. showing Lincoln winning the North-east and West, Breckinridge winning the South, Douglas winning Missouri, and Bell winning Virginia, West Virginia, and Kentucky." src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/1860_Electoral_Map.png/300px-1860_Electoral_Map.png" width="300" height="161" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1860_Electoral_Map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
In 1860, northern electoral votes (shown in red) put Lincoln into the White House.</div>
</div>

</div>
<p>On November 6, 1860, Lincoln was elected the sixteenth president of the United States, beating Democrat Stephen A. Douglas, John C. Breckinridge of the Southern Democrats, and <a href="/wiki/John_Bell_(Tennessee_politician)" title="John Bell (Tennessee politician)">John Bell</a> of the new <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Union_Party_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Union Party (United States)">Constitutional Union Party</a>. He was the first Republican president, winning entirely on the strength of his support in the North; he was not on the ballot in ten states in the South, and won only two of 996 counties in all the Southern states.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln received 1,866,452 votes, Douglas 1,376,957 votes, Breckinridge 849,781 votes, and Bell 588,789 votes. The electoral vote was decisive: Lincoln had 180 and his opponents added together had only 123. Turnout was 82.2&#160;percent, with Lincoln winning the free Northern states. Douglas won Missouri, and split New Jersey with Lincoln.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> Bell won Virginia, Tennessee, and Kentucky, and Breckinridge won the rest of the South.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> There were <a href="/wiki/Electoral_fusion" title="Electoral fusion">fusion tickets</a> in which all of Lincoln's opponents combined to form one ticket in New York, New Jersey, and Rhode Island, but even if the anti-Lincoln vote had been combined in every state, Lincoln still would have won a majority in the electoral college.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>As Lincoln's election became evident, secessionists made clear their intent to leave the Union.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> On December 20, 1860, South Carolina took the lead; by February 1, 1861, Florida, Mississippi, Alabama, Georgia, Louisiana, and Texas followed.<sup id="cite_ref-Donald.2C_p._267_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donald.2C_p._267-127"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> Six states then adopted a constitution and declared themselves to be a sovereign nation, the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America">Confederate States of America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Donald.2C_p._267_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donald.2C_p._267-127"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> The upper South (Delaware, Maryland, Virginia, North Carolina, Tennessee, Kentucky, Missouri, and Arkansas) listened to, but initially rejected, the secessionist appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> President Buchanan and President-elect Lincoln refused to recognize the Confederacy.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> The Confederatcy selected <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis">Jefferson Davis</a> as their provisional President on February 9, 1861, .<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>There were attempts at compromise, such as the <a href="/wiki/Crittenden_Compromise">Crittenden Compromise</a>, which would have extended the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> line of 1820,<sup id="cite_ref-White360-361_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White360-361-132"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> and which some Republicans even supported. Lincoln rejected the idea, saying, "I will suffer death before I consent...to any concession or compromise which looks like buying the privilege to take possession of this government to which we have a constitutional right."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln, however, did support the <a href="/wiki/Corwin_Amendment">Corwin Amendment</a> to the Constitution, which had passed in Congress and protected slavery in those states where it already existed.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> A few weeks before the war, he went so far as to pen a letter to every governor asking for their support in ratifying the Corwin Amendment as a means to avoid secession.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abraham_lincoln_inauguration_1861.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large crowd in front of a large building with many pillars." src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Abraham_lincoln_inauguration_1861.jpg/220px-Abraham_lincoln_inauguration_1861.jpg" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abraham_lincoln_inauguration_1861.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Lincoln's inaugural on March 4, 1861 at the United States Capitol</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>En route to his inauguration, President-elect Lincoln <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Plot" title="Baltimore Plot">evaded possible assassins</a> in Baltimore, who were uncovered by Lincoln's head of security, <a href="/wiki/Allan_Pinkerton">Allan Pinkerton</a>, and on February 23, 1861, arrived in disguise in Washington, D.C., which was placed under substantial military security.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln directed his <a href="/wiki/Lincoln%27s_first_inaugural_address" title="Lincoln's first inaugural address">inaugural speech</a> to the South, saying, "We are not enemies, but friends. We must not be enemies...The mystic chords of memory, stretching from every battlefield, and patriot grave, to every living heart and hearthstone, all over this broad land, will yet swell the chorus of the Union, when again touched, as surely they will be, by the better angels of our nature."<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> The failure of the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Conference_of_1861">Peace Conference of 1861</a> rendered legislative compromise practically implausible. By March 1861, no leader of the insurrection had proposed rejoining the Union on any terms. Lincoln and nearly every Republican leader agreed a dismantling of the Union could not be tolerated.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_begins">War begins</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War">American Civil War</a></div>
<p>The commander of Fort Sumter, South Carolina sent a request for provisions to Washington, and the execution of Lincoln's order to meet that request was seen by the secessionists as an act of war.<sup id="cite_ref-Donald_1996.2C_p._293_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donald_1996.2C_p._293-139"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> On April 12, 1861, Confederate forces fired on Union troops at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fort_Sumter" title="Battle of Fort Sumter">Fort Sumter</a>, forced them to surrender, and began the war.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Allan_Nevins">Allan Nevins</a> argued that the newly inaugurated Lincoln miscalculated in believing that he could preserve the Union,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> and future general <a href="/wiki/William_Tecumseh_Sherman">William Tecumseh Sherman</a>, then a civilian, visited Lincoln in the White House during inauguration week and was "sadly disappointed" at Lincoln's seeming failure to realize that "the country was sleeping on a volcano" and that the South was preparing for war.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> Historian and noted Lincoln biographer, <a href="/wiki/David_Herbert_Donald">David Herbert Donald</a>. concluded Lincoln fairly estimated the events leading to the initiation of war. "His repeated efforts to avoid collision in the months between inauguration and the firing on Ft. Sumter showed he adhered to his vow not to be the first to shed fraternal blood. But he also vowed not to surrender the forts. The only resolution of these contradictory positions was for the confederates to fire the first shot; they did just that."<sup id="cite_ref-Donald_1996.2C_p._293_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donald_1996.2C_p._293-139"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>On April 15, Lincoln called on the states to send detachments totaling 75,000 troops<sup id="cite_ref-Oates.2C_p._226_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oates.2C_p._226-143"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> to recapture forts, protect the capital, and "preserve the Union," which, in his view, still existed intact despite the actions of the seceding states.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> These events forced the states to choose sides. Virginia declared its secession, after which the Confederate capital was moved from Montgomery, Alabama to <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond</a>. North Carolina, Tennessee, and Arkansas also voted for secession over the next two months. Missouri, Kentucky, and Maryland threatened secession,<sup id="cite_ref-Oates.2C_p._226_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oates.2C_p._226-143"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> but neither they nor the slave state of Delaware seceded.</p>

<p>Troops headed south towards Washington, D.C., to protect the capital in response to Lincoln's call. On April 19, secessionist mobs in Baltimore that controlled the rail links <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_riot_of_1861" title="Baltimore riot of 1861">attacked Union troops</a> traveling to the capital. <a href="/wiki/George_William_Brown">George William Brown</a>, the Mayor of Baltimore, and other suspect Maryland politicians were arrested and imprisoned as Lincoln suspended the writ of <i><a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus">habeas corpus</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Merryman">John Merryman</a>, a leader in the secessionist group in Maryland, petitioned Chief Justice Roger Taney to issue a writ of habeas corpus, saying Lincoln's action of holding Merryman without a hearing was unlawful. Taney issued the writ, thereby ordering Merryman's release, but Lincoln ignored it.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assuming_command_for_the_Union_in_the_war">Assuming command for the Union in the war</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln_and_the_Civil_War">Abraham Lincoln and the Civil War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RunningtheMachine-LincAdmin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A group of men sitting at a table as another man creates money on a wooden machine." src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/RunningtheMachine-LincAdmin.jpg/300px-RunningtheMachine-LincAdmin.jpg" width="300" height="220" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RunningtheMachine-LincAdmin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.17/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
"Running the 'Machine'&#160;": An 1864 political cartoon takes a swing at Lincoln's administration – featuring William Fessenden, Edwin Stanton, William Seward, Gideon Welles, Lincoln and others.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lincoln encountered an unprecedented crisis, and he responded as Commander in Chief, using unprecedented powers. He expanded his war powers, and imposed a blockade, disbursed funds before appropriation by Congress, and suspended the writ of <i>habeas corpus</i>, arresting and imprisoning thousands of suspected Confederate sympathizers without warrant.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>The war effort was the source of continued disparagement of Lincoln, and dominated his time and attention. From the start, it was clear that bipartisan support would be essential to success in the war effort, and any manner of compromise alienated factions on both sides of the aisle, such as the appointment of Republicans and Democrats to command positions in the Union Army.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Copperheads_(politics)" title="Copperheads (politics)">Copperheads</a> and other opponents of the war criticized Lincoln for refusing to compromise on the slavery issue. Conversely, the Radical Republicans criticized him for moving too slowly in abolishing slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In August 1861, General <a href="/wiki/John_C._Fr%C3%A9mont">John C. Frémont</a> created controversy on the Republican side when he issued, without consulting Lincoln, a proclamation of martial law in Missouri. He declared that any citizen found bearing arms could be court-martialed and shot, and that slaves of persons aiding the rebellion would be freed. Charges of negligence in his command of the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_the_West">Department of the West</a> were compounded with allegations of fraud and corruption. Lincoln's efforts to rein him in were futile, and he was given another command in November. This decision, in part, prevented the secession of Kentucky while incurring the violence in the North.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>The war assumed foreign policy implications in 1861 when <a href="/wiki/James_Murray_Mason" title="James Murray Mason">James Mason</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Slidell">John Slidell</a>, ministers of the Confederacy to Great Britain and France, boarded the British ship <i>Trent</i> in Havana, Cuba. The U.S. Navy illegally intercepted the <i>Trent</i> on the high seas and seized the two Confederate envoys; Britain protested vehemently while the northern Americans cheered. Lincoln managed to resolve the issue by releasing the two men.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Lincoln's foreign policy approach had been initially hands off, due to his inexperience; he left most diplomacy appointments and other foreign policy matters to his Secretary of State, William Seward. Seward's initial reaction to the <i>Trent</i> affair, however, was too bellicose, so Lincoln also turned to Sen. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sumner">Charles Sumner</a>, the chairman of the Senate Foreign Relations Committee and an expert in British diplomacy.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>Despite his lack of expertise in military affairs, Lincoln studied books from the Library of Congress and devoured the telegraphic reports. He kept close tabs on all phases of the military effort, consulted with governors, and selected generals based on their past success. In January 1862, after numerous complaints about the running of the War Department, Lincoln dismissed the inept Secretary <a href="/wiki/Simon_Cameron">Simon Cameron</a> and replaced him with <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Stanton" class="mw-redirect" title="Edwin Stanton">Edwin Stanton</a>, a reputedly successful leader.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> In terms of war strategy, Lincoln articulated two priorities: to ensure that Washington was well defended, and to conduct an aggressive war effort that would satisfy the demand in the North for prompt, decisive victory; major Northern newspaper editors expected victory within 90 days.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> Two days per week, Lincoln would meet with his cabinet in the afternoon, and occasionally his wife would force him to take a carriage ride because she was concerned he was working too hard.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> Lincoln grasped the need to control strategic points, such as the Mississippi River and the fortress city of Vicksburg, and understood the importance of defeating the enemy's army, rather than simply capturing territory.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_McClellan">General McClellan</span></h3>
<p>One of Lincoln's Democrat commanders, General <a href="/wiki/George_B._McClellan">George B. McClellan</a>, refused to move against the enemy as aggressively as Lincoln directed. He became general-in-chief of all the Union armies in the wake of a Union defeat at the <a href="/wiki/First_Battle_of_Bull_Run">First Battle of Bull Run</a> and after the retirement of the aged <a href="/wiki/Winfield_Scott">Winfield Scott</a> in late 1861.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> McClellan, a young <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">West Point</a> graduate and railroad executive, took several months to plan and attempt his <a href="/wiki/Peninsula_Campaign">Peninsula Campaign</a>, with the objective of capturing Richmond by moving the <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Potomac">Army of the Potomac</a> by boat to the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Peninsula" title="Virginia Peninsula">peninsula</a> and then overland to Richmond. McClellan's repeated delays frustrated Lincoln and Congress, as did his position that no troops were needed to defend Washington. Lincoln insisted on holding some of McClellan's troops in defense of the capital; McClellan, who consistently overestimated the strength of Confederate troops, blamed this decision for the ultimate failure of the Peninsula Campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<p>Lincoln removed McClellan as general-in-chief and appointed <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wager_Halleck">Henry Wager Halleck</a> after McClellan's <i>Harrison's Landing Letter</i>, in which he offered unsolicited political advice to Lincoln urging caution in the war effort.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> McClellan's letter incensed Radical Republicans, who successfully pressured Lincoln to appoint <a href="/wiki/John_Pope_(military_officer)" title="John Pope (military officer)">John Pope</a>, a Republican, as head of the new <a href="/wiki/Army_of_Virginia">Army of Virginia</a>. Pope complied with Lincoln's strategic desire to move toward Richmond from the north, thus protecting the capital from attack.
